Sale of Land with Per-Unit Price and Buyer's Option
Ruling on sale of land with price per cubit and buyer's option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's khiyar options when land sold with a per-unit price is found to be less or more than stated, and apply the ruling to similar transactions.

Core Idea
When each unit is priced individually, the buyer gets a khiyar if the actual quantity differs.

In the previous lesson, we examined a lump-sum sale where the buyer had no option if the land was larger. Here, we contrast that with a per-unit pricing arrangement. The seller explicitly prices each cubit, making the quantity a principal element. Consequently, if the actual land varies, the buyer is granted a khiyar to either accept the land at the proportional price or cancel the sale entirely. This ruling illustrates how the specificity of pricing terms can shift contractual rights under Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract specification
The seller states: 'each cubit for a dirham'. This makes the price per unit, not a lump sum. The quantity becomes a condition of the deal.
Contrast with lump-sum sale where the total price is for the entire property as described.
2
Analyse the buyer's options when land is less
If the land is found to be less than stated, the buyer may either take it for its share of the price (i.e., pay less for the actual smaller quantity) or cancel the sale entirely.
The buyer is not forced to take the smaller land at the original total price.
3
Analyse the buyer's options when land is more
If the land is found to be more than stated, the buyer may either take all of it at the per-unit price (paying for the actual larger quantity) or cancel the sale.
The buyer is not forced to pay extra for the excess; he has a choice.
4
Contrast with lump-sum sale
In a lump-sum sale, if the land is larger, the seller may take the excess without extra payment. Here, because each unit is priced, the buyer's option protects him from being forced to accept a different quantity.
The key distinction is the pricing structure: lump-sum vs. per-unit.
5
Derive the underlying principle
When the price is linked to a unit, the quantity becomes a condition (shart) of the contract. Any discrepancy triggers khiyar for the buyer to ensure fairness.
This reflects the Hanafi concern for gharar (uncertainty) in essential terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال بعتكها على انها مائة ذراع بمائة دراهم كل ذراع بدرهم فوجدها اقل من ذلك فالمشترى بالخيار ان شاء اخذها بحصتها من الثمن وان شاء تركها وان وجدها اكثر من ذلك فالمشترى بالخيار ان شاء اخذها بكل ذراع بدرهم وان شاء نقض البيع اور کہا کہ اس زمین کو آپ سے بیچتا ہوں اس طرح کہ سو گز ہے سو درہم کے بدلے میں، ہرگز ایک درہم کے بدلے، پھر اس کو کم پایا تو مشتری کو اختیار ہے اگر چاہے تو اس زمین کو اس کے حصے کے مطابق شمن سے لے اور اگر چاہے تو چھوڑ دے۔ اور اگر زمین کو زیادہ پایا تو مشتری کو اختیار ہے اگر چاہے تو پوری زمین کو لے ہرگز ایک درہم کے بدلے میں اور چاہے تو بیع توڑ دے۔
And if he says, 'I sell you this (land) on the condition that it is one hundred cubits for one hundred dirhams, each cubit for a dirham,' and he finds it less than that, the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes it for its share of the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. And if he finds it more than that, the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes it all, each cubit for a dirham, and if he wishes, he nullifies the sale. And he said: I sell this land to you on the condition that it is one hundred yards for one hundred dirhams, each yard for one dirham. Then he found it less, so the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes this land for its share of the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. And if he finds the land more, the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes the entire land, each yard for a dirham, and if he wishes, he nullifies the sale.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that contrasts with the previous one. Here, the seller explicitly prices each cubit (each cubit for a dirham), making the cubit a principal (asl). Therefore, if the land is less than stated, the buyer may take it at a reduced price or cancel. If it is more, the buyer has the option to take all at the per-unit price (paying the full price for the actual total) or cancel the sale. The seller cannot force the sale because the agreement was based on a specific quantity. This gives the buyer full khiyar in both scenarios.

Key Terms
جملة (jumla): Total; the overall price versus per-unit price.
نقض البيع (naqd al-bay'): To nullify the sale or break the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If land is sold as '100 cubits for 100 dirhams, each cubit for 1 dirham' and is found to be 110 cubits, what are the buyer's options?
A) The buyer must take all 110 cubits for 110 dirhams
B) The buyer can take 100 cubits for 100 dirhams and leave the rest
C) The buyer can take all 110 cubits for 110 dirhams or cancel the sale
D) The buyer must cancel the sale

Key Takeaway
When land is sold with a per-unit price, any difference in quantity gives the buyer an option to either take it at the proportional price or cancel the sale.

Buying cloth or land with specified measurement
Ruling on deficiency or excess in measured goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when measured goods are deficient or excessive, and apply the rule to similar sales.

Core Idea
Excess belongs to buyer; deficiency gives buyer option, not automatic price reduction.

This passage from the matn presents a key axiom in Hanafi sales law: when a measured item like cloth or land is sold for a lump sum, the measurement is a description, not a price unit. The ruling clarifies what happens if the actual measure differs from what was stated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract structure
The sale is for a specific item (a cloth or land) described by a total measurement (10 cubits) for a lump sum price (10 dirhams). Each cubit does not have a separate price.
This is a lump-sum sale (safqah wahidah), not a per-unit sale.
2
Apply the deficiency scenario
If the actual measurement is less than stated, the buyer has a choice: take it at full price or cancel the sale. This is an option (khiyar) due to deficiency in description.
The price does not reduce proportionally.
3
Apply the excess scenario
If the actual measurement is more than stated, the buyer takes it all at the agreed price. The seller has no option to cancel or demand more money.
The extra belongs to the buyer because the price was for the whole item.
4
Contrast with per-unit sale
If the price were per unit (e.g., 1 dirham per cubit), the total would adjust automatically. In a lump-sum sale, measurement is merely a description.
This distinction is crucial for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اشترى ثوبا على انه عشرة اذرع بعشرة دراهم او ارضا على انها مائة ذراع بمائة دراهم فوجدها اقل من ذلك فالمشترى بالخيار ان شاء اخذها بجملة الثمن وان شاء تركها وان وجدها اكثر من الذراع الذى سماه فهى للمشترى ولا خيار للبائع
Whoever buys a cloth on the condition that it is ten cubits for ten dirhams, or land on the condition that it is one hundred cubits for one hundred dirhams, and finds it less than that, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes he leaves it. And if he finds it more than the cubits he specified, then it belongs to the buyer, and the seller has no option.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Sharh Ul Sameri on the sale of measured items like cloth or land. The ruling: when a buyer purchases an item described by a specific total measurement (e.g., ten cubits) for a lump sum price, and the actual measurement is less, the buyer has an option (khiyar) to either take the entire item at the agreed price or cancel the sale. If the actual measurement is more, the entire item belongs to the buyer at the same price, and the seller cannot cancel. The reason, as explained in the commentary, is that the measurement here is treated as a description (sifah), not as the basis of the price. The price is for the whole item, regardless of exact length; therefore, any excess goes to the buyer, and any deficiency gives the buyer a choice but does not reduce the price. This differs from a sale where each unit has a separate price, as in the next passage.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhira'): A unit of length, a cubit, used as a measurement for cloth or land.
خيار (khiyar): Option or right to choose given to a party in a contract due to a defect or deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases land described as 100 cubits for 100 dirhams, and finds it is 90 cubits, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is automatically void.
B) The buyer must take it for 90 dirhams.
C) The buyer may take it for 100 dirhams or cancel.
D) The seller can cancel.

Key Takeaway
In a lump-sum sale of measured goods, deficiency gives buyer an option; excess belongs to buyer; seller has no option.

Sale with Per-Unit Pricing: Deficiency and Excess
Ruling when each unit has a separate price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's options when land sold with per-unit price is found to be deficient or excessive.

Core Idea
When price is per unit, measurement is the basis; buyer has option on excess or deficiency.

This lesson examines a specific sale scenario: a seller offers land stating '100 cubits for 100 dirhams, each cubit for 1 dirham.' The key distinction is that the price is allocated per unit. If the actual measurement differs, the buyer—not the seller—holds the option. Understanding this ruling clarifies the difference between lump-sum and per-unit pricing in Hanafi sales law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pricing structure
Note that the contract specifies 'each cubit for a dirham.' This makes the cubit the basis (asl) of the price, not merely a description.
2
Consider deficiency scenario
If the land is found to have fewer cubits (e.g., 80 instead of 100), the buyer may either take the actual land at a proportional price (80 dirhams) or cancel the sale.
The seller cannot force the original price.
3
Consider excess scenario
If the land is found to have more cubits (e.g., 110 instead of 100), the buyer may take the entire land by paying for all cubits at the per-unit rate (110 dirhams) or cancel the sale.
The seller has no option; the buyer decides.
4
Compare with lump-sum pricing
In contrast, if the price were a lump sum for the whole (e.g., 100 dirhams for 100 cubits without per-unit allocation), the seller would have no option on excess, and deficiency would give the buyer only proportional reduction.
This distinction is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انها مائة ذراع بمائة درهم كل ذراع بدرهم فوجدها ناقصة فهو بالخيار ان شاء اخذها بحصتها من الثمن وان شاء تركها وان وجدها زائدة كان المشترى بالخيار ان شاء اخذ الجميع كل ذراع بدرهم وان شاء فسخ البيع
That it is one hundred cubits for one hundred dirhams, each cubit for a dirham, and he finds it deficient, then he has the option: if he wishes he takes it by its share of the price, and if he wishes he leaves it. And if he finds it excessive, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes he takes all, each cubit for a dirham, and if he wishes he voids the sale.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This ruling statement is the second scenario: when the seller specifies a price per unit (each cubit for a dirham), the measurement becomes the basis (asl) of the price, not just a description. Therefore, if the land is less than the stated cubits, the buyer may take the actual land at a reduced price proportional to the measured cubits (e.g., 80 cubits for 80 dirhams), or cancel. If the land is more (e.g., 110 cubits), the buyer may take the whole land by paying for all cubits at the per-unit rate (110 dirhams) or cancel the sale. The seller has no unilateral option because the contract is based on actual measured units. The commentary distinguishes this from the previous case by noting that here each cubit is priced separately, whereas in the previous case the price was a lump sum for the whole item without per-unit allocation.

Key Terms
كل ذراع بدرهم (kullu dhira'in bi-dirham): Each cubit for a dirham — specifying a per-unit price, making the cubit the basis (asl).
بحصتها من الثمن (bi-hissatiha min al-thaman): By its share of the price — proportional reduction corresponding to the actual measurement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If land is sold with the condition 'each cubit for 1 dirham, total 100 cubits for 100 dirhams' and it is found to be 110 cubits, what can the buyer do?
A) Only take 100 cubits for 100 dirhams.
B) Take all 110 cubits for 110 dirhams or cancel.
C) The seller can cancel.
D) The buyer must take all for 100 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
In per-unit pricing, the buyer alone has the option to cancel or accept the actual measurement at the per-unit rate.

Sale of a Bundle with Specified Count: Ruling on Deficiency and Excess
Ruling on deficiency/excess in bundle with per-item price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a bundle sold with a specified count and per-item price is found to be deficient or excessive, and explain why the sale is valid in deficiency but invalid in excess due to selection dispute.

Core Idea
Deficiency validates the sale by share; excess invalidates it due to potential selection dispute.

A bundle of cloths is sold with a stated count and per-item price. What happens if the actual number differs? The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between deficiency and excess. This lesson unpacks the logic behind that distinction and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract terms
The seller specifies both the total number of items (e.g., ten cloths) and a per-unit price (each cloth for ten dirhams). This creates a clear expectation of quantity and value.
2
Recall the ruling for deficiency
If the buyer finds fewer cloths (e.g., nine), the sale is valid for the actual number at the proportional price. There is no dispute because the fewer items are clearly the same as those sold.
The sale is sahih, and the seller refunds the price of the missing cloth.
3
Recall the ruling for excess
If the buyer finds more cloths (e.g., eleven), the sale is invalid (fasid). The extra cloth cannot be unilaterally assigned; the seller and buyer may disagree on which cloth is the extra, leading to a dispute over selection.
This differs from cases where items are identical (like land), where excess does not cause such a dispute.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The key is the potential for dispute (tanāzu'). When the items are not perfectly identical (cloths may vary in quality), the extra item creates uncertainty about which item is surplus, making the contract invalid. Deficiency does not create this problem because there is no extra item to select.
If the items were identical (e.g., identical cubits of land), the excess would not invalidate the contract.
5
Apply to similar scenarios
This ruling applies to any bundle of non-identical items sold with a specified count and per-item price. For identical items sold as a unit (e.g., a bundle of identical books), the rule may differ.
Always check if the items are fungible or distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال بعت منك هذه الرزمة على انها عشرة اثواب بمائة درهم كل ثوب بعشرة فان وجدها ناقصة جاز البيع بحصته وان وجدها زائدة فالبيع فاسد
And if he said, 'I sold you this bundle on the condition that it is ten cloths for one hundred dirhams, each cloth for ten,' and he finds it deficient, then the sale is valid by its share; and if he finds it excessive, then the sale is invalid.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This ruling statement addresses the sale of a bundle (رزمة) of cloths where the seller specifies both the total number (ten cloths) and a per-unit price (each cloth for ten dirhams). If the buyer finds fewer cloths (e.g., nine), the sale is valid for the actual cloths at the proportional price (nine for ninety dirhams) because there is no dispute about selection—the fewer items are clearly the same as those sold. However, if the buyer finds more cloths (e.g., eleven), the sale is invalid (fasid) because the excess cloth would need to be separated, and the seller and buyer might disagree on which cloth is the extra one, leading to dispute. The commentary notes that when individual items differ in quality, such selection disputes render the contract invalid. This is unlike the previous cases where the items (cubits of land) are identical, so no dispute arises.

Key Terms
رزمة (ruzma): A bundle or package, here containing cloths (ثواب).
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): An invalid sale that is not permissible and must be annulled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sale of a bundle said to contain ten cloths at ten dirhams each, if eleven cloths are found, why is the sale invalid?
A) Because the price is too low.
B) Because the excess cloth causes uncertainty in selection.
C) Because the seller did not know the count.
D) Because the buyer cannot afford the extra.

Key Takeaway
In a bundle sale with per-item price, deficiency validates the sale by share, but excess invalidates it due to selection dispute.

Sale of a house includes its structure
Ruling that building is included in house sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the building structure is included in the sale of a house and explain the role of custom.

Core Idea
When you buy a house, you buy its building too — custom includes what is permanently attached.

This short lesson introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi sales law: the sale of a house automatically includes the building and all its permanent fixtures. The ruling is based on custom ('urf), which determines what is considered part of the house. Understanding this rule helps prevent disputes and ensures clarity in real estate transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is selling a house (dar). The ruling is that the building structure (bina') is automatically included in the sale.
The ruling is unconditional – no need for explicit mention.
2
Understand what 'structure' includes
Structure means walls, roof, doors, windows, and other permanent attachments. Items that are customarily considered part of the house are included.
Custom ('urf) is the key determinant.
3
Distinguish from movable items
Movable items like furniture, carpets, or appliances are not automatically included. They require explicit agreement.
This avoids confusion between fixture and chattel.
4
Recognize the role of custom
Hanafi fiqh uses custom to define what is part of the house. Different regions may have different customs, so the ruling adapts.
Custom must be consistent and well-established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع دارا دخل بناؤها في البيع
And whoever sells a house, its structure enters into the sale.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This brief ruling statement establishes that when a house (دار) is sold, everything that is part of the building structure (بناؤها) — such as walls, roof, doors, windows — is automatically included in the sale, even if not explicitly mentioned. The commentary explains that this applies to items that are attached to the house in a permanent manner and are customarily considered part of it (urf). This is based on the principle that in sales, custom ('urf) determines what is included. The ruling is straightforward: the building is inseparable from the house, so no separate mention is needed.

Key Terms
دار (dar): House, including the land and building.
بناؤها (bina'uha): Its structure — the building, walls, roof, and other permanent attachments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, they must be separately mentioned.
B) Yes, because they are part of the structure.
C) Only if the buyer asks.
D) No, doors are moveable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the sale of a house automatically includes all permanent attached structures, determined by custom.

Sale of Land by the Cubit: Option When Quantity Differs
States the ruling for sale of land by the cubit when quantity differs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's options when a land sold per cubit is found to be more or less than stipulated.

Core Idea
When land is sold per unit, the total price adjusts, but the buyer gets an option if the quantity differs.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi ruling on the sale of land by the cubit. When the seller stipulates a per-unit price, the total price adjusts to the actual measure. However, any discrepancy in quantity triggers the buyer's option (khiyar) to either accept the land at the adjusted price or rescind the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pricing structure
The seller says 'each cubit for a dirham,' making the price per unit independent of total quantity.
2
Case of shortage
If the land is less than stipulated, the buyer has the option to take it at the reduced price or cancel.
3
Case of excess
If the land is more, the buyer has the option to take all at the per-unit rate or cancel; he cannot take only part.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
The sale is per cubit, so each cubit is a separate sale; thus the buyer's consent for extra cubits is required.

ٹکڑے کی مجموعی قیمت لگا رہا ہے یا ہر ہر گز کی الگ الگ قیمت لگا رہا ہے۔ اگر پورے ٹکڑے کی قیمت لگا رہا ہے تو گز زیادہ ہو یا کم پورے ٹکڑے کی پوری قیمت دینی ہوگی۔ اور اگر ہر ہر گز کی قیمت لگا رہا ہے تو گز کے حساب سے اس کی قیمت طے کی جائے گی ۔ ان ہی اصولوں پر یہ سب مسائل متفرع ہیں ۔ [۸۳۵] (۱۶) میں نے آپ سے یہ گٹھری بیچی اس طرح کہ دس کپڑے ہیں سو درہم کے بدلے، ہر کپڑا دس درہم کے بدلے، پس اگر ان کو کم پایا نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر گیارہ کپڑے نکلے تو ایک کپڑا کے چھانٹنے میں اختلاف ہوگا۔ بائع گھٹیا دینا چاہے گا اور مشتری اعلی لینا چاہے گا اور اختلاف و نزاع ہوگا اس لئے اس صورت میں بیع فاسد ہوگی۔ انون کپڑے یا کسی چیز کے افراد میں تفاوت ہو اور اس کو چھانٹنے میں اختلاف ہو سکتا ہو تو بیع فاسد ہوگی۔ انت الرزمة : گھری۔ ثوب : کپڑا، تھان۔
That it is one hundred cubits for one hundred dirhams, each cubit for a dirham; if he finds it short, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes it for its proportionate share of the price; if he wishes, he leaves it. If he finds it excessive, the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes all, each cubit for a dirham; if he wishes, he rescinds the sale. [835] (16) And if he said: I sold you this bundle on condition that it is ten garments for one hundred dirhams, each garment for ten dirhams; if he finds it short, the sale is valid for its share; if he finds it excessive, the sale is void. [836] (17) In cloth and land, the cubit is free; but if the description is made the basis, then a separate price is assigned against it. Here when the seller said that each cubit is for one dirham, he made the cubit the basis and now each cubit will be for one dirham. Now the sale is not of the entire piece of land but it is the sale of each cubit. Therefore, whatever cubits there are, that many dirhams become obligatory. If there are fewer, then fewer dirhams; if more, then more dirhams. However, in the case of fewer cubits, the buyer did not get the promised land, so his interest is reduced, thus he has the option to take or leave. The principle: the cubit is a description, but if it is made the basis, then each cubit gets its own price. It must be seen whether the seller is fixing the total price of the entire piece or a separate price for each cubit. If he fixes the price of the entire piece, then whether the cubits are more or less, the full price of the entire piece must be paid. And if he fixes the price for each cubit, then the price is determined by the cubits. All these issues branch out from these principles. [835] (16) I sold you this bundle on the basis that it is ten garments for one hundred dirhams, each garment for ten dirhams; so if he finds them short, the sale is valid for their share; if he finds them excessive, the sale is void. In the bundle there were ten garments, each of different quality. The seller now said: I sell you the entire bundle on condition that it is ten garments and each garment is worth ten rupees. So if fewer garments are found, e.g., nine garments come out, then at ten dirhams each, he takes nine garments for ninety dirhams. And because the garments are fewer, there is no need for selection and no dispute. And if eleven garments come out, then there will be dispute in selecting one garment: the seller will want to give an inferior one and the buyer will want to take a superior one, leading to dispute and conflict, so in this case the sale is void. In garments or any item where individuals differ and selection can cause dispute, the sale is void. 'Ar-razma': bundle. 'Thawb': garment, piece of cloth.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings about sale when the stipulated quantity differs from actual. The first ruling concerns a sale where the entire land is described by a measure (dhirā') per unit price. Here, the price is per cubit, so the total price adjusts to the actual cubits. If the land is less than stipulated, the buyer has the option to take it at the reduced price or cancel. This is because he was expecting a certain size but got less, so he may not want it. If it is more, he has the option to take all at the per-unit rate or cancel; he cannot take only part because the price is per unit and he must take all or none. The second ruling concerns a bundle of garments sold with a specified number and per-unit price. If fewer garments are found, the sale is valid for the actual number at the per-unit price, with no option because he got exactly what he paid for. If more garments are found, the sale becomes void due to potential dispute over selecting which garment to exclude; since garments are not identical, each party would prefer a different one, leading to conflict, so the contract is invalid. The commentary further explains the underlying principle: if the description (like cubit or garment) is made the basis of the contract, each unit has its own price; if the entire item is sold as a whole, the total price is fixed regardless of quantity. These rulings are standard in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhirā'): A cubit, a unit of length used for pricing per unit in land sale.
خيار (khiyār): Option; right to rescind or affirm a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling, if a buyer purchases land per cubit and finds it is 10 cubits short, what are his options?
A) He must take it at the reduced price
B) He may take it at the reduced price or cancel
C) He may demand the seller provide the missing 10 cubits
D) The sale is void automatically

Key Takeaway
In per-unit sales, any deviation in quantity gives the buyer the option to accept the adjusted total or rescind.

Sale of a Bundle of Garments: Valid When Less, Void When More
States the ruling for sale of a bundle of non-identical garments when quantity differs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling when a bundle of non-identical garments is found to have more or fewer items than stated.

Core Idea
Excess in non-homogeneous items voids the sale due to selection dispute.

This lesson covers the second ruling in the passage: the sale of a bundle of non-identical garments. When the seller stipulates a specific number and per-item price, a shortage poses no problem, but an excess leads to dispute over which item to exclude, rendering the sale void (fasid).

Guided Path
1
Identify the sale type
A bundle of garments sold as a lot with a specified count and per-item price, but the garments are not identical.
2
Case of shortage
If fewer garments are found, the sale is valid for the actual number at the per-item price; no dispute arises because no selection is needed.
3
Case of excess
If more garments are found, the sale becomes fasid (void) because selecting which item to exclude leads to conflict between buyer and seller.
4
Understand the reason
The garments are non-homogeneous; the seller wants to give a cheap one, the buyer wants an expensive one, causing gharar (uncertainty).

Automatic Inclusion of Fixtures in Sale: Building and Trees
States that building is included in sale of house, and trees in sale of land without specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that permanently attached fixtures are automatically included in the sale of the principal object, and identify the exception of explicit exclusion.

Core Idea
What is permanently attached to the sold item passes with it without mention – custom makes it part of the deal.

When buying a house or land, what exactly are you purchasing? Does the sale automatically include the building and trees? The Hanafi school has clear answers based on custom ('urf) and the nature of attachment. This lesson explains the principle behind automatic inclusion and its application in sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sales
Note the two cases: sale of a house and sale of land. The ruling treats them similarly.
2
Extract the ruling for each
For house: the building (bina') is included. For land: date-palms (nakhl) and other trees (shajar) are included.
Both are included even if not named.
3
Identify the underlying principle
The principle is that anything permanently attached to the sold object is considered part of it and passes automatically.
Permanence distinguishes fixtures from movable crops.
4
Note the exception
If the seller explicitly excludes the fixtures, they are not included. Otherwise, they pass.
Exclusion must be clear and specific.
5
Apply the rule to other cases
Consider other permanently attached items: doors, windows in a house; wells, fences in land. They follow the same rule.
Custom determines what is 'permanently attached'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع دارا دخل بناؤها في البيع وان لم يسمه [۱۸۸۳۷) ومن باع ارضا دخل ما فيها من النخل والشجر في البيع وان لم يسمه [ ١٩٨٣٨) کسی نے گھر خریدا تو اس کی دیوار بیع میں داخل ہوگی چاہے اس کا نام نہ لیا ہو۔ اصول جو چیز بیچ کے ساتھ دائمی طور پر متصل ہو وہ چیز بیچ میں بغیر اس کا نام لئے ہی داخل ہو جائے گی۔ ۱۸۳۷ (۱۸) کسی نے زمین بیچی تو اس میں جو کھجور کے درخت اور دوسرے درخت ہیں سب بیچ میں داخل ہوں گے چاہے ان کا نام نہ لیا ہو ر زمین بیچی تو اس میں جو کھجور وغیرہ کے درخت ہیں وہ سب خود بخود بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے چاہے بیع کرتے وقت یہ نہ کہا ہو کہ زمین کے ساتھ درخت بھی خریدتا ہوں۔ کھجور کے درخت اور دوسرے درخت ہمیشہ کے لئے زمین پر لگے رہتے ہیں۔ کھیتی اور کا شتکاری کی طرح چار چھ ماہ میں کاٹ نہیں لیتے اس لئے وہ زمین کے جزء کی طرح ہیں۔ اس لئے جب زمین کی بیع کی تو درخت بھی خود بخود بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے۔ ہاں ان کو با ضابطہ بیع سے الگ کر دے تو پھر بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوں گے۔ جو چیز طبیع کے ساتھ دائمی طور پر متصل ہوں وہ چیزیں بیچ میں بغیر ان کا نام لئے بھی داخل ہو جائیں گی۔ نخل : کھجور کا درخت۔
Whoever sells a house, its building is included in the sale even if he did not name it. [837] Whoever sells land, what is in it of date-palms and trees is included in the sale even if he did not name it. [838] If someone bought a house, its wall enters the sale even if he did not name it. Principle: Whatever is permanently attached to the sold item enters the sale without naming it. (1837) (18) If someone sold land, the date-palms and other trees that are in it are all included in the sale, even if he did not name them. If land is sold, the date-palms etc. trees are automatically included in the sale even if at the time of sale he did not say, 'I buy the trees along with the land.' Date-palms and other trees remain permanently fixed on the land; they are not cut in four to six months like crops and cultivation, so they are like a part of the land. Therefore, when land is sold, the trees are automatically included. Yes, if he explicitly excludes them, then they are not included in the sale. Things that are permanently attached to the land enter the sale without naming. Nakhil: date-palm tree.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings on what is automatically included in a sale. First, when a house is sold, its building (walls, roof, etc.) is included even if not mentioned. Second, when land is sold, all date-palms and other trees rooted in the land are included. The principle is that anything permanently attached to the sold item is considered part of it by custom ('urf) and is included without specification. The commentary elaborates that trees are like a part of the land because they are long-lasting, unlike crops which are seasonal. Therefore, they pass automatically with the land unless explicitly excluded. The Hanafi school bases this on the idea that permanent fixtures follow the principal object.

Key Terms
بناء (bina'): Building or structure affixed to land, automatically included in sale of house.
نخل (nakhl): Date-palm trees, considered part of the land due to permanence.
شجر (shajar): Trees in general, other than date-palms, also included in land sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells land without mentioning the trees, do the date-palm trees on it pass to the buyer?
A) Yes, they are automatically included
B) No, they remain the seller's property
C) Only if the buyer expressly asked for them
D) Only if they are mature trees

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, whatever is permanently attached to the sold item (building to house, trees to land) passes automatically in the sale unless explicitly excluded.

Crops Not Included in Land Sale Unless Specified
States that crops are not included in land sale without specification, with hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that crops are not included in a land sale unless explicitly named and apply the principle of temporary versus permanent attachments.

Core Idea
Crops are temporary attachments—they remain the seller's unless explicitly included.

When buying agricultural land, a common assumption is that everything on it, including growing crops, transfers to the buyer. Hanafi fiqh, however, draws a clear distinction between permanent fixtures (like buildings) and temporary attachments (like crops). This lesson establishes the basic rule: crops are not automatically part of the land sale; they belong to the seller unless the buyer explicitly stipulates otherwise. The ruling is anchored in a hadith of the Prophet (PBUH) and reflects a practical principle that prevents disputes over harvest ownership.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
First, memorize the core ruling: crops are not included in the sale of land unless specifically named in the contract.
This is a clear, operational rule from the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the reasoning
Recognize the underlying principle: crops are temporarily attached to the land—they will be harvested within months—so they are considered movable property, not part of the land.
The same logic applies to any seasonal or temporary growth.
3
Link to the hadith proof
The hadith about selling a pollinated date-palm states that the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise. This hadith is the primary evidence for the rule, showing that even attached fruit is not automatically included.
The hadith is authentic (Bukhari and Muslim).
4
Apply to other cases
Generalize from the hadith to all crops (wheat, barley, vegetables, etc.). The same principle applies to any temporary vegetation standing on the land at the time of sale.
The rule is mutlaq (unrestricted) and covers all similar situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يدخل الزرع في بيع الارض الا بالتسمية [ ۸۳۹ (۲۰) کاشتکاری زمین کی بیع میں داخل نہیں ہو گی مگر اس کا نام لینے کے بعد ۔ جب تک یہ نہ کہے کہ کاشتکاری بھی زمین کے ساتھ خریدتا ہوں تب تک زمین کی بیچ میں کاشتکاری داخل نہیں ہوگی۔ کا شتکاری اور کھیتی زمین کے ساتھ ہمیشہ کے لئے متصل نہیں ہے، وہ تو دو چار ماہ میں کٹ جائے گی اور الگ ہو جائے گی ، وہ تو وقتی طور پر زمین کے ساتھ متصل ہے اس لئے زمین کی بیچ میں کاشتکاری داخل نہیں ہوگی جب تک کہ اس کا بیچ میں نام نہ لیا جائے۔ حدیث میں اس کا اشارہ ہے عن ابن عمر ان رسول الله الله قال من باع نخلا قد ابرت فقدرها للبائع الا ان يشترط المبتاع (الف) (مسلم شریف ، باب من باع نخلا عليها تمرج ثانی ص ۱۰ نمبر ۱۵۴۳ر بخاری شریف، باب من باع نخلا قد ابرت ادارضا مزروعته او با جارة ص ۲۹۳ نمبر ۲۲۰۴) اس حدیث میں کھجور کا درخت بیچا تو فرمایا کھجور کے بائع کے لئے ہیں مگر یہ مشتری خرد نے کے وقت شرط لگائے ۔ اور کھجور درخت پر وقتی طور پر لگا رہتا ہے پھر کٹ جاتا ہے جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ وقتی طور پر کوئی چیز بیع کے ساتھ متصل ہو تو وہ چیز بیچ میں داخل نہیں ہوگی مگر یہ کہ شرط کرے۔ جو چیز بیع کے ساتھ وقتی طور پر متصل ہو وہ چیز بیچ میں داخل نہیں ہوگی۔ الزرع : کھیتی ، کاشتکاری۔
And crops are not included in the sale of land except by naming. [839] (20) Cultivation is not included in the sale of land except after naming it. As long as he does not say, 'I also buy the cultivation along with the land,' the cultivation is not included in the sale of land. Cultivation and crops are not permanently attached to the land; they are cut in a few months and separated. They are temporarily attached, so they are not included in the sale of land unless named. The hadith indicates this: from Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever sells a date-palm that has been pollinated, its fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it.' (Muslim, Bukhari). In this hadith, the date-palm tree was sold; he said the dates belong to the seller unless the buyer stipulated at the time of purchase. And dates are temporarily on the tree, then cut, so it shows that anything temporarily attached to the sold item is not included in the sale unless stipulated. Something that is temporarily attached is not included in the sale. 'Az-zar': cultivation, farming.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that crops (like wheat, barley, etc.) are not automatically included in the sale of land. The reason is that crops are temporary: they will be harvested in a few months and are not permanently part of the land. Therefore, they remain the property of the seller unless specifically mentioned in the contract. The commentary cites a hadith about selling a date-palm with fruit: the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it. This illustrates the principle that temporary attachments follow the original owner. The Hanafi school applies this to all crops and seasonal produce. Thus, if one wants to buy the crops along with the land, he must explicitly include them in the sale.

Key Terms
زرع (zar'): Crops or cultivation that are standing in the field, not yet harvested.
نخل قد أبرت (nakhlun qad ubrira): A date-palm that has been pollinated; its fruit is in the process of developing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone buys agricultural land without mentioning the wheat crop growing on it, who owns the crop?
A) The buyer, because it is on his land
B) The seller, because crops are not included automatically
C) It is shared equally between buyer and seller
D) It depends on who planted it

Key Takeaway
Crops and temporary attachments are not automatically included in the sale of land; explicit stipulation is required.

Sale of Land Includes Trees Automatically
Ruling that trees are included in land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the inclusion of trees in a land sale and explain the basis for automatic inclusion based on permanent attachment.

Core Idea
Trees permanently attached to land are automatically included in its sale, unless explicitly excluded.

When buying land, does the sale include the trees already growing on it? In Hanafi fiqh, the answer is clear: trees that are permanently fixed to the land are considered part of the land itself. This short passage from a classical Hanafi text explains the ruling and its rationale, distinguishing between permanent fixtures and temporary crops.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of land. The ruling is that all trees (date palms and other permanent trees) are included in the sale automatically, regardless of whether they are mentioned in the contract.
The ruling is explicit: the seller does not need to list the trees.
2
Understand the condition for automatic inclusion
Trees must be permanently attached to the land. The text contrasts them with crops that are cut after a few months. Permanent attachment makes them akin to a part of the land.
Temporary crops like wheat are not included automatically.
3
Grasp the rationale
The rationale is that trees are like a part of the land because they remain fixed and are not harvested temporarily. Thus, they transfer with the land.
This is based on customary understanding (urf) and legal analogy.
4
Note the exception
The parties can exclude trees by explicit stipulation in the contract. If the seller says 'I sell the land without the trees,' the trees remain with the seller.
This respects party autonomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن باع ارضا دخل ما فيها من النخل والشجر في البيع وان لم يسمه۔ کسی نے زمین بیچی تو اس میں جو کھجور کے درخت اور دوسرے درخت ہیں سب بیچ میں داخل ہوں گے چاہے ان کا نام نہ لیا ہو۔ کھجور کے درخت اور دوسرے درخت ہمیشہ کے لئے زمین پر لگے رہتے ہیں۔ کھیتی اور کاشتکاری کی طرح چار چھ ماہ میں کاٹ نہیں لیتے اس لئے وہ زمین کے جزء کی طرح ہیں۔ اس لئے جب زمین کی بیع کی تو درخت بھی خود بخود بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے۔ ہاں ان کو با ضابطہ بیع سے الگ کر دے تو پھر بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوں گے۔
And whoever sold land, what is in it of date palms and trees enters into the sale even if he did not name them. Someone sold land, so the date palm trees and other trees that are in it are all included in the sale even if he did not name them. Date palm trees and other trees remain permanently attached to the land. They are not cut like crops in four or six months, so they are like a part of the land. Therefore, when the land is sold, the trees also automatically enter into the sale. Yes, if he explicitly excludes them from the sale, then they will not be included.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of land. The author states that when land is sold, all trees (including date palms) that are permanently attached to it are included in the sale automatically, whether or not the buyer mentions them. The reason given is that trees are permanently fixed to the land and are not harvested like crops; they are considered part of the land itself. Therefore, they transfer with the land. However, the ruling allows the parties to exclude the trees by explicit stipulation, in which case they remain with the seller.

Key Terms
دخل (dakhala): Included automatically in the sale without explicit mention.
نخل (nakhl): Date palm trees, specifically mentioned as an example of permanently attached trees.
شجر (shajar): General term for trees, including date palms and others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if someone sells land without mentioning the trees, what happens to the trees?
A) They remain with the seller unless specifically named.
B) They are included in the sale automatically.
C) The buyer must pay extra for them.
D) The sale is invalid if trees are not mentioned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, trees permanently attached to land are automatically part of the land sale unless explicitly excluded.

Crops Not Included in Land Sale
Ruling that crops are not included in land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on crops in land sale, explain the reasoning based on temporary attachment, and apply the hadith proof to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Crops are not included in land sale unless explicitly named—temporary attachment does not transfer.

When you buy land, what about the crops growing on it? Are they yours too? The Hanafi school has a clear answer based on the nature of attachment and a key hadith. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its reasoning, and the prophetic proof that shapes it.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the rule: crops (zar') are not included in the sale of land unless they are specifically named in the contract (tasmiyah).
The default is exclusion; inclusion requires explicit stipulation.
2
Understand the reasoning
The reason is that crops are only temporarily attached to the land; they will be harvested within a few months. Permanent fixtures (like trees) transfer, but temporary ones do not.
Temporal attachment vs. permanent attachment is the key distinction.
3
Analyze the proof
The hadith of Ibn Umar: selling a pollinated date palm gives the fruit to the seller unless the buyer stipulates. This shows that temporary produce on a sold item remains with the seller by default.
The analogy: fruit on trees is like crops on land; both are temporary.
4
Identify cross-madhhab difference
Some Shafi'i scholars might include crops if they are still unripe, but Hanafis stick to the principle of temporary attachment requiring explicit inclusion.
The practical difference: in Shafi'i, the buyer might get the crop automatically in certain cases.
5
Apply to a scenario
If someone buys a field with wheat stalks ready to harvest, and no mention of the wheat, the seller keeps the wheat. The buyer must negotiate separately or stipulate.
This ruling protects sellers from inadvertently giving away valuable crops.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يدخل الزرع في بيع الارض الا بالتسمية۔ کاشتکاری زمین کی بیع میں داخل نہیں ہو گی مگر اس کا نام لینے کے بعد۔ جب تک یہ نہ کہے کہ کاشتکاری بھی زمین کے ساتھ خریدتا ہوں تب تک زمین کی بیع میں کاشتکاری داخل نہیں ہوگی۔ کاشتکاری اور کھیتی زمین کے ساتھ ہمیشہ کے لئے متصل نہیں ہے، وہ تو دو چار ماہ میں کٹ جائے گی اور الگ ہو جائے گی، وہ تو وقتی طور پر زمین کے ساتھ متصل ہے اس لئے زمین کی بیع میں کاشتکاری داخل نہیں ہوگی جب تک کہ اس کا بیع میں نام نہ لیا جائے۔ حدیث میں اس کا اشارہ ہے عن ابن عمر ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال من باع نخلا قد ابرت فقدرها للبائع الا ان يشترط المبتاع (الف) (مسلم شریف، باب من باع نخلا عليها تمرج ثانی ص ۱۰ نمبر ۱۵۴۳ر بخاری شریف، باب من باع نخلا قد ابرت ادارضا مزروعته او با جارة ص ۲۹۳ نمبر ۲۲۰۴) اس حدیث میں کھجور کا درخت بیچا تو فرمایا کھجور کے بائع کے لئے ہیں مگر یہ مشتری خرد نے کے وقت شرط لگائے۔ اور کھجور درخت پر وقتی طور پر لگا رہتا ہے پھر کٹ جاتا ہے جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ وقتی طور پر کوئی چیز بیع کے ساتھ متصل ہو تو وہ چیز بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوگی مگر یہ کہ شرط کرے۔
And crops are not included in the sale of land except by naming. Cultivation will not be included in the sale of land except after naming it. As long as he does not say, 'I also buy the cultivation along with the land,' until then cultivation is not included in the sale. Cultivation and crops are not permanently attached to the land; they will be cut in two or four months and become separate. They are only temporarily attached to the land, so cultivation is not included in the sale of land unless its name is mentioned in the sale. The hadith indicates this: From Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, 'Whoever sold a date palm that has been pollinated, its fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates [otherwise].' (Muslim, Bukhari). In this hadith, when a date palm was sold, he said the dates belong to the seller unless the buyer stipulates. And dates are temporarily attached to the tree and then cut, from which it is understood that if something is temporarily attached to the sold item, it is not included in the sale unless he stipulates.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of land with crops. The author states that crops (like wheat, vegetables) are not included in the sale of land unless they are specifically named in the contract. The reasoning is that crops are only temporarily attached to the land; they will be harvested within a short time and are not considered a permanent part of the land. The author supports this with a hadith from Ibn Umar regarding the sale of a date palm with fruit: the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise. This analogy shows that temporary attachments (like fruit on a tree or crops on land) are not automatically transferred with the sale.

Key Terms
زرع (zar'): Crops or cultivation that is temporarily on the land.
تسمية (tasmiyah): Specifically naming the item in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a crop included in the sale of land?
A) Automatically if the crop is still growing.
B) Only if it is specifically named in the contract.
C) Never, because crops are considered separate property.
D) If the buyer pays an extra amount.

Key Takeaway
Temporarily attached items like crops are not automatically part of a land sale; explicit naming is required.

Fruit Belongs to Seller Unless Stipulated
Ruling on fruit when selling a tree

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fruit when selling a tree and explain the proof from the hadith of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
Fruit on a tree is not automatically included when selling the tree; it belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise.

When you buy a fruit tree, does the fruit come with it? This might seem obvious, but Islamic law treats the fruit as a separate commodity. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling based on a clear prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is selling a tree (e.g., date palm) that has fruit. The ruling is that the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates its inclusion in the sale contract.
The ruling applies to all trees, not just date palms.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar (ra) recorded in Bukhari and Muslim: 'Whoever sold a date palm that has been pollinated, its fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it.'
The hadith is ahad (khabar wahid), thus zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit statement 'its fruit belongs to the seller' directly proves the ruling. The exception 'unless the buyer stipulates' shows that the default ownership is with the seller.
The wording is clear and unambiguous in establishing the default.
4
Note the condition
The buyer can stipulate (yashrut) inclusion of the fruit. If he does, the fruit becomes part of the sale. Without stipulation, the fruit remains the seller's property.
This is a condition (shart) in the contract that changes the default.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
The position is from Zahir al-Riwayah, mufta bihi, and considered muttafaq alayh across schools in its essence. The reasoning is that fruit is temporarily attached; it will be harvested and is not a permanent part of the tree.
This distinguishes it from crops growing in sold land (see previous lesson).

If he sold a date palm or a tree that has fruit, its fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it. Someone sold a date palm tree or another tree that had fruit, so the fruit will be for the seller unless the buyer makes a condition for it. Footnote: He said: Whoever sold a date palm that has fruit, its fruit is for the seller unless the buyer stipulates it. If the tree was sold, the fruit will not be included in the sale. Yes, if the buyer stipulates that he is also buying the fruit along with the tree, then the fruit will be included in the sale. The fruit is not permanently attached to the tree; rather, after a few months it will be cut and separated from the tree. And from the hadith in issue number 19, we learned that if something is not permanently attached to the sold item, it will not be included in the sale. The hadith passed: from Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever sold a date palm that has been pollinated, its fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it.' (Bukhari, Muslim) In this hadith, it is mentioned that if a date palm tree is sold, the dates will not be included in the sale.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of a tree that bears fruit. The author rules that when a tree is sold, any fruit on it at the time of sale remains the property of the seller, unless the buyer explicitly stipulates in the contract that the fruit is included. The reasoning is that fruit is only temporarily attached to the tree; it will eventually be harvested and is not a permanent part. The author supports this with the same hadith from Ibn Umar that was used for crops: the tree itself is sold, but the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise. This establishes the general principle that temporary attachments are not automatically transferred.

Key Terms
نخل (nakhl): Date palm tree, specific example used in the hadith.
ثمرة (thamarah): Fruit that is on the tree at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The fruit of a tree is not automatically transferred in a sale unless the buyer stipulates its inclusion, based on the hadith of Ibn Umar.

Seller Must Cut Fruit and Deliver Sold Item
Consequence: seller ordered to remove fruit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when fruit is not included in a tree sale and explain why the seller must cut and deliver the tree.

Core Idea
If fruit is not sold with the tree, the seller must cut it to free the buyer's property.

This passage explains the practical consequence when fruit is excluded from a tree sale. It establishes that the seller's continued possession of the fruit on the buyer's tree is impermissible, and therefore the seller must remove it immediately. The ruling is supported by the hadith prohibiting profit without liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The seller's obligation to cut fruit and deliver the tree. The ruling: wajib (obligatory).
2
Understand the reason: twofold ownership conflict
The fruit remains the seller's property but occupies the buyer's tree. Since ownership must be separate, the seller's possession is an encroachment.
The principle: one cannot benefit from another's property without liability.
3
Locate the dalil and its application
The hadith: 'No profit without liability' (la ribha ma lam yadman). The seller profits from his fruit while occupying the tree without bearing liability for the tree, so it is impermissible.
Hadith from Tirmidhi.
4
Derive the consequence: obligation to cut and deliver
To end the encroachment, the seller must cut the fruit (thus separating his property) and hand over the tree to the buyer. This is the legal consequence.
If the seller fails to do so, he may be compelled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للبائع اقطعها وسلم المبيع۔ بائع سے کہا جائے گا کہ پھل کو کاٹو اور مبیع کو سپرد کرو۔ جب پھل درخت کی بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوا تو پھل بائع کا رہا اور درخت مشتری کا ہو گیا۔ اور بائع کی ملکیت نے مشتری کی ملکیت کو مشغول کر رکھا ہے حالانکہ دونوں کی ملکیت الگ الگ ہونی چاہئے ۔ اس لئے بائع سے کہا جائے گا کہ پھل کاٹو اور درخت خالی کر کے مشتری کے حوالے کر دو۔ بغیر ضمان کے دوسرے کی چیز سے فائدہ اٹھانا جائز نہیں ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن عبد الله بن عمر ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لا يحل سلف و بيع ولا شرطان في بيع ولا ربح مالم يضمن ، ولا بيع مالیس عندک (ب) ( ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی کراہیتہ بیع مالیس عنده ص ۲۳۳ نمبر ۱۲۳۴ رسن سیھتی ، باب الشرط الذي يفسد البيع ج خامس ص ۵۴۸، نمبر ۱۰۸۳۸) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ جس چیز کا ضمان نہ دیتا ہو اس سے فائدہ اٹھانا حلال نہیں۔ اس لئے بائع سے کہا جائے گا کہ مشتری کے درخت سے مزید فائدہ نہ اٹھاؤ اور پھل کاٹ کر درخت مشتری کے حوالے کر دو۔ بغیر اجازت اور بغیر ضمان کے دوسرے کی ملکیت سے فائدہ اٹھانا جائز نہیں ہے۔
And it will be said to the seller: Cut it (the fruit) and hand over the sold item. It will be said to the seller: Cut the fruit and hand over the sold item. Since the fruit was not included in the sale of the tree, the fruit remains the seller's and the tree becomes the buyer's. And the seller's ownership is occupying the buyer's ownership, whereas both ownerships should be separate. Therefore, the seller will be told to cut the fruit and vacate the tree and deliver it to the buyer. It is not permissible to benefit from another's property without liability. A hadith states: From Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'It is not permissible to combine a loan and a sale, nor two conditions in one sale, nor profit on something for which you are not liable, nor to sell what is not with you.' (Tirmidhi). This hadith indicates that it is not lawful to benefit from something for which you do not bear liability. Therefore, the seller will be told not to take further benefit from the buyer's tree, and to cut the fruit and hand over the tree to the buyer. It is not permissible to benefit from another's property without permission and without liability.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the practical consequence of the previous ruling. Since the fruit remains the seller's property but is physically on the buyer's tree, the seller is instructed to cut the fruit and deliver the tree to the buyer. The author explains that this is necessary because the seller's ownership should not continue to occupy the buyer's property. He supports this with a hadith that prohibits benefiting from something for which one does not bear liability (i.e., profit without risk). The seller is essentially using the buyer's tree to hold his fruit, which is not allowed without permission. Therefore, the court (or the parties) will order the seller to remove the fruit promptly.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability for loss; indicates responsibility for the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
If fruit is not included in selling a tree, the seller must cut and deliver the tree to avoid occupying the buyer's property without liability.

Selling Fruit Before and After Ripeness
Ruling on selling fruit regardless of ripeness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of fruit regardless of ripeness and explain the buyer's immediate obligation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, selling fruit is valid whether ripe or unripe; the buyer must take delivery immediately.

This lesson addresses a fundamental ruling in Hanafi sales (buyu'): the validity of selling fruit that may not yet be ripe. While some schools condition ripeness, the Hanafi school permits the sale of unripe fruit, emphasizing that the contract is sound and the buyer is bound to take possession forthwith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of fruit (ثمرة). The ruling is that it is permissible (جاز) regardless of whether ripeness has appeared or not.
The text covers both scenarios: fruit before ripeness and after.
2
Note the absence of the ripeness condition
The Hanafi school does not require that the fruit be ripe at the time of sale. This contrasts with other schools that may condition validity on ripeness.
Ripeness (صلاح) is not a shart (condition) for the sale to be valid.
3
Understand the buyer's immediate obligation
The text explicitly states that the buyer is obliged (واجب) to take delivery immediately (فی الحال). This implies that the fruit is ready for transfer and the contract is binding.
Immediate delivery prevents gharar (uncertainty) and ensures the sale is not deferred.
4
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school may require ripeness for the sale of fruit to be valid, based on hadith prohibiting the sale of fruit before it ripens. The Hanafi school interprets such ahadith as recommendations, not absolute prohibitions.
This ikhtilaf is a legitimate difference in usul methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن باع ثمرة لم يبد صلاحها او قد بدا جاز۔ کسی نے پھل بیچے جس کی صلاحیت ظاہر ہو چکی ہو یا ظاہر نہ ہوئی ہو تو بیع جائز ہے، اور مشتری پر واجب ہوگا اس کو فی الحال
And whoever sold fruit whose ripeness has not appeared or has appeared, it is permissible. Someone sold fruit whose ripeness has appeared or has not appeared, the sale is valid, and it will be obligatory upon the buyer to [take delivery] immediately.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of fruit itself (not attached to a tree). The author states that selling fruit is valid whether its ripeness (salah) has become evident or not. This means that one can sell unripe fruit (like green dates) or ripe fruit; both are permissible. The buyer is then obligated to take delivery immediately. The text seems to be cut off, but the ruling is clear: the sale is valid in both cases.

Key Terms
صلاح (salah): Ripeness or readiness for consumption, often indicated by change in color or softness.
بدا صلاحها (bada salahaha): Its ripeness has appeared – the fruit has started to ripen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Sale of Tree with Fruit: Fruit Belongs to Seller
Ruling on sale of tree with fruit and remedy for overlapping ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of a fruit-bearing tree and apply the exception when the buyer stipulates inclusion of the fruit.

Core Idea
The fruit of a sold tree belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise — a direct ruling from the Prophet (ﷺ).

This lesson addresses a common transaction: selling a fruit tree that currently bears fruit. The Hanafi school, following the Sunnah, rules that the fruit is not automatically included in the sale. This protects both parties: the seller retains his crop, and the buyer can stipulate to purchase it if desired. The wisdom lies in the fruit being a separate, removable asset.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on selling a palm tree (or any fruit tree) with fruit on it. The default ruling: the fruit belongs to the seller, not the buyer.
The act is a valid sale (sahih) of the tree; the fruit is a separate property.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar (رضي الله عنهما) is quoted: the Prophet (ﷺ) said, 'Whoever sells a pollinated palm tree, its fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates.' This is a Sunnah ahad (thubut zanni) with clear meaning (dalala zanni).
The hadith specifies 'pollinated' (ubirrat), but Hanafis apply the ruling to all fruit, whether pollinated or not.
3
Understand the exception
The buyer can stipulate (yashrut) that the fruit is included. If stipulated, the fruit becomes part of the sale and transfers to the buyer.
Stipulation must be explicit at the time of contract.
4
Apply the remedy for overlapping ownership
Since the fruit remains the seller's property but is physically on the buyer's tree, the seller is ordered to cut the fruit and deliver the tree empty to the buyer.
This prevents the seller from benefiting from the buyer's property without guarantee (daman).
5
Note the underlying principle
Items not permanently attached (muttaṣil dāʾiman) to the sold object are not included in the sale. Fruit is temporally attached and will be separated.
This principle is derived from issue 19 in the same text.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of sales (buyu') regarding the sale of a tree with fruit. The author states the matn: when a palm tree or any fruit-bearing tree is sold, the fruit on it at the time of sale belongs to the seller, not the buyer, unless the buyer stipulates that he is buying the fruit as well. This is based on the hadith of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) where the Prophet (ﷺ) ruled that the fruit of a pollinated palm tree belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise. The commentary explains the logic: the fruit is not permanently attached to the tree; it is a separate commodity. This principle was earlier established in issue number 19: any item not permanently attached to the sold object is not included in the sale. Therefore, the buyer does not automatically own the fruit. The remedy for the overlapping ownership is that the seller is ordered to cut the fruit and deliver the tree empty to the buyer. This is because the seller's ownership of the fruit occupies the buyer's ownership of the tree, and it is not permissible to benefit from another's property without guarantee (daman). The second hadith about combining a loan and sale, etc., supports that one cannot profit from something without bearing its risk. Hence, the seller must remove the fruit promptly.

Key Terms
نخل (nakhl): Date palm tree specifically, but by extension any fruit-bearing tree.
ابرت (ubirrat): Pollinated; the fruit has been fertilized and begins to develop.
مبتاع (mubta'): Buyer; the one who purchases.

Key Takeaway

8 min
Selling Fruit Before Ripening: Validity and Conditions
Ruling on selling fruit with condition of immediate cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit before ripening and explain why a condition to leave the fruit on the tree renders the sale fasid.

Core Idea
Selling unripe fruit is valid, but immediate cutting is obligatory; a condition to delay cutting makes the sale fasid.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on selling fruit before or after ripening. While a hadith prohibits such sales, the Hanafi school interprets it as advisory. The matn from Al-Quduri clarifies the ruling: the sale is valid, but the buyer must cut the fruit immediately. Any condition to leave the fruit on the tree introduces uncertainty, making the contract corrupt (fasid). Understanding this distinction is key to grasping Hanafi contract theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states: 'Whoever sells fruit, whether its ripeness has appeared or not, the sale is valid.' The act is selling fruit; the hukm is jā'iz (valid).
2
Note the obligatory condition
The matn continues: 'It is obligatory upon the buyer to cut it immediately.' This is a wājib arising from the contract, not a condition of validity.
3
Identify the invalidating condition
The matn says: 'If he stipulates leaving it on the palm, the sale is corrupt (fāsid).' Stipulating a delay in cutting introduces gharar (uncertainty) and invalidates the contract.
4
Examine the dalīl
The hadīth of Ibn 'Umar prohibits selling fruit until ripeness appears. The Hanafis use the context of disputes (hadīth of Zayd ibn Thābit) to interpret this as advisory (not a binding prohibition).
The prohibition is overridden by istihsān because the sale is otherwise valid and disputes can be avoided by cutting immediately.
5
Understand the Hanafi istihsān
Imam Abū Hanīfah holds that selling unripe fruit is permissible because it is the seller's property and can be used in some way. The prophetic advice is precautionary, not a bar of invalidity.

[841] (22) And whoever sells fruit, whether its ripeness has appeared or not, the sale is valid. And it is obligatory upon the buyer to cut it immediately. If he stipulates leaving it on the palm, the sale is corrupt. (Urdu commentary: If someone sells fruit whose fitness has become apparent or not, the sale is valid, and the buyer is obligated to cut it immediately. If he stipulates leaving it on the tree, the sale becomes invalid. It is permissible to sell fruit that has become usable, e.g., edible or able to be fed to animals; rather, the hadith encourages selling fruit when it becomes usable, not before. In the hadith: From Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah prohibited selling fruit until its ripeness becomes apparent, prohibiting both the seller and the buyer. (Bukhari, Muslim) In this hadith, he encouraged not selling fruit before ripening and before being safe from blights. However, according to the Hanafis, selling fruit before it is safe from blights is permissible if one wishes, because (1) it is the seller's property and he can sell it, and it will be useful in some way, otherwise the buyer would not buy it. But based on the hadith, it is not commendable. (2) In Bukhari it is stated that many disputes between buyers and sellers came before the Prophet, so he advised as a counsel: Do not sell fruit before it becomes usable, so that the buyer does not suffer loss and delay payment. The hadith from Zayd ibn Thabit: People in the time of the Prophet used to buy fruit, and when the harvest came and payment was due, the buyer would claim that blight, disease, etc. had struck, using as excuses. When disputes increased, the Prophet said: If you do not stop disputing, then do not buy until the fruit's ripeness appears. This was advisory due to their many disputes. This indicates that the original ruling is permissibility, and this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifah. The buyer will be told: Cut the fruit immediately and hand the tree back to the seller.)
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the second ruling on fruit sales: selling fruit whether ripened or not. The matn states that the sale is valid in both cases, but the buyer is obligated to cut the fruit immediately. If the buyer stipulates leaving the fruit on the tree to ripen further, the sale becomes corrupt (fasid). The Hanafi commentary explains that selling fruit before it is fully ripe is valid because it is the seller's property and can be used in some way. However, the hadith prohibiting sale until ripeness is interpreted as advisory (nadb) due to the many disputes that arose when fruit was sold before ripening and then blight struck. Imam Abu Hanifah holds that the original ruling is permissibility, but it is better to delay sale until ripeness to avoid disputes. The buyer is ordered to cut immediately to prevent ambiguity in ownership. The key distinction: the sale itself is valid, but a condition to leave fruit on the tree makes it fasid because it involves uncertainty (gharar) about delivery and future condition.

Key Terms
بدو صلاحها (buduww salahiha): The appearance of ripeness or fitness; when fruit has become edible or usable.
فاسد (fasid): A corrupt sale that is not valid due to an illegal condition, but can be rectified by removing the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a buyer of unripe fruit stipulates that the fruit should remain on the tree for a month?
A) The sale is valid and the condition is binding.
B) The sale becomes corrupt (fasid) because of the condition.
C) The sale is void (batil) from the start.
D) The buyer must cut immediately but the condition is ignored.

Key Takeaway
Selling unripe fruit is valid in the Hanafi school, but a condition to postpone cutting renders the sale corrupt (fasid) due to uncertainty.

Selling Fruits Before Ripeness: Hanafi Ruling
States the Hanafi ruling on selling unripe fruits with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruits before ripeness and explain how the hadith is interpreted as advisory rather than prohibitive.

Core Idea
Fruit before ripening can be sold, but it is disliked; the buyer must cut it immediately.

This passage from the commentary on the Book of Sales addresses the permissibility of selling fruits before they have ripened. While the hadith forbids such sales, the Hanafi school interprets this prohibition as a recommendation to avoid disputes, not as a strict ruling. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping Hanafi sales law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being sold? Unripe fruits. What is the Hanafi ruling? The sale is valid (sahih) but disliked (makruh tanzih).
The sale is not invalid, but it is not recommended.
2
Understand the condition of immediate cutting
The buyer is obliged to cut the fruit immediately upon purchase. If the seller stipulates leaving the fruit on the tree, the sale becomes fasid.
This condition prevents uncertainty and potential damage.
3
Examine the primary dalil
The hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar reports that the Prophet prohibited selling fruits until ripeness appears. Hanafis note the context: frequent disputes.
The hadith is sahih but its meaning is not absolute according to Hanafis.
4
Understand the Hanafi interpretation
The prohibition is seen as advisory (irshad) based on the hadith of Zayd ibn Thabit, where the Prophet gave advice due to many disputes, not a binding prohibition.
This is the key difference with the Shafi'i school.
5
Note the practical consequence
The seller can sell, the buyer must cut immediately. A condition to leave renders the sale invalid. The ruling is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.
This avoids both complete prohibition and uncertainty.

The Sharh al-Thamiri, Part Two, 17. Book of Sales. The sale: and it is obligatory upon the buyer to cut them immediately, so if he stipulates leaving them on the palm, the sale becomes invalid. Explanation: So if he stipulates leaving them on the tree, the sale becomes invalid. Fruits that have become useful, e.g., can be eaten or fed to animals, selling them is permissible. Rather, in hadith there is encouragement that when fruit becomes useful then sell; before that selling is not good. A hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah prohibited selling fruits until their ripeness appears, prohibiting the seller and the buyer. (Bukhari, Muslim). This hadith encourages not selling before ripening and before being safe from blights. But if one wants to sell before being safe from blights, he can sell; according to the Hanafis it is permissible. (1) It is the seller's property, and he can sell his property. And it will be used in some way, that is why the buyer is buying it. Therefore, even if fruit is not useful, the seller can sell it. However, based on the hadith, it is not good. (2) In Bukhari's hadith, it is clarified that many disputes between seller and buyer came before the Prophet, so he advised not to sell until useful so that the buyer does not incur loss and delays payment. From Zayd ibn Thabit: people in the time of the Prophet used to sell fruits, and when they harvested and payment became due, the buyer would claim that the fruit was struck by disease, blight, etc., as excuses. The Prophet, due to the frequency of disputes, said: 'If you do not cease, then do not sell until the fruit's ripeness appears,' as a suggestion because of their many disputes. (Bukhari). This hadith clarifies that they used to sell before usefulness; then when calamity occurred and fruits were destroyed, the buyer would delay payment. When many disputes came before the Prophet, he advised not to sell before usefulness. This means that in principle selling is permissible, and this is the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa. Therefore, the seller can sell his property. The buyer will be told to immediately cut the fruits and hand over the tree to the seller. Footnote (a): He prohibited selling fruits until their ripeness appears, i.e., until they become useful, and forbade the seller and buyer. (b) People in the Prophet's time used to sell fruits. When their clamor increased and demands came, the buyer would say the fruit was affected by disease, etc., as excuses. When disputes became many, he said: 'If you do not refrain, then do not sell until the ripeness of the date appears, i.e., near ripening.' This was a suggestion because of their many disputes.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the Book of Sales. It presents the Hanafi ruling on selling fruits before they have ripened or become useful. The author states the basic rule: the buyer must cut the fruits immediately; if a condition is made to leave them on the tree, the sale becomes fasid (invalid). Then he explains that if the fruits have already become useful (e.g., edible), selling them is permissible. The evidence from hadith (Bukhari and Muslim) shows that the Prophet prohibited selling until ripeness appears, but the Hanafis interpret this as a recommendation (irshad) rather than a strict prohibition. The reasoning is that the seller owns the fruit and can sell his property; the prohibition was given because of frequent disputes between buyers and sellers when the fruit was damaged before harvest. Imam Abu Hanifa's school holds that selling before ripeness is permissible (ja'iz) but makruh tanzih (disliked) based on the hadith. The buyer is required to cut the fruit immediately.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit, especially dates
بدو صلاحها (badu salahiha): The appearance of ripeness or usefulness of fruit
مكروه (makruh): Disliked; an action whose avoidance is rewarded but does not incur sin

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Haram and the sale is invalid
B) Permissible but disliked, and the sale is valid

D) Invalid unless the buyer cuts them immediately

Key Takeaway
Selling fruits before ripeness is permissible (ja'iz) in the Hanafi school but disliked (makruh tanzih), and the buyer must cut them immediately; a stipulation to leave them invalidates the sale.
