Exception (Istithna') in Fruit Sales
States the ruling on exception in fruit sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling fruit with an exception and explain why it is fasid using the hadith 'No profit without liability'.

Core Idea
Selling fruit with an exception that leaves the remainder unknown is invalid due to gharar and the principle of no profit without liability.

This passage presents a key ruling in Hanafi sales law: the prohibition of selling fruit while making an exception of a known quantity. The ruling is rooted in the principle that one cannot benefit from another's property without assuming liability (daman). We will unpack the ruling, its reason, and the hadith evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific transaction being discussed? The seller sells fruit and excepts known ratls from the sale. The ruling: it is not allowed (la yajuz), meaning the sale is fasid (invalid).
2
Determine the reason (illah) for the ruling
The text gives the reason: the tree remains the seller's property, and the buyer benefits from the fruit without liability. This creates uncertainty (gharar) about the exact quantity sold.
The exception makes the remainder unknown, leading to gharar.
3
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The hadith 'No profit without liability' (لا ربح ما لم يضمن) is cited from Tirmidhi. This hadith establishes that one cannot profit from something without bearing the risk of loss.
The hadith is ahad (single transmission) but accepted in fiqh.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah (how the hadith applies)
In this transaction, the seller retains ownership of the tree (and thus the excepted fruit) but the buyer takes the sold fruit without liability for the excepted part. The seller profits from the buyer's payment without bearing risk on the excepted portion.
This violates the hadith principle.
5
Note the consequence and practical outcome
The sale is fasid (invalid). If concluded, it must be rescinded. This ruling protects against uncertainty and unfair gain.
The sale is not batil (void) but fasid (invalid due to defect), which can be rectified by removing the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۸۴۲ (۲۳) ولا يجوز ان يبيع ثمرة ويستثنى منها ارطالا معلومة. کیونکہ درخت بائع کی ملکیت ہے اور دوسرے کی ملکیت سے بغیر ضمان کے فائدہ اٹھانا جائز نہیں ہے۔ پہلے ترمذی کی حدیث نمبر ۱۲۱۴، ولا ربح ما لم يضمن گزر چکی ہے۔
[842] (23) It is not allowed to sell fruit and except from it known ratls. Because the tree is the property of the seller and it is not allowed to benefit from another's property without liability. Previously, the hadith of Tirmidhi no. 1214, 'There is no profit without liability,' has passed.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on exception (istithna') in fruit sales. The author states that it is not permissible to sell fruit and make an exception of a known quantity (e.g., 'I sell all the fruit except 100 kg'). The reason is that the tree belongs to the seller, and it is not allowed to benefit from another's property without assuming liability. This is based on the hadith principle 'There is no profit without liability.' If the exception leaves the remainder unknown, the sale becomes fasid (invalid). However, if the exception is known and does not cause uncertainty, it may be permissible.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception; a clause in a contract that excludes certain items from the sale.
رطل (ratl): A unit of weight; here used for a known quantity.
ضمان (daman): Liability; responsibility for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it not allowed to sell fruit and make an exception of known ratls?
A) Because the buyer cannot take possession
B) Because the seller benefits from another's property without liability
C) Because the fruit is not yet ripe
D) Because the sale is conditional

Key Takeaway
Selling fruit with an exception that leaves the remainder unknown is fasid due to gharar and the principle of no profit without liability.

The Shafi'i Prohibition on Selling Unripe Fruits
Presents the Shafi'i ruling and its evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits selling unripe fruits while the Shafi'i school prohibits it, and evaluate the evidence each school uses.

Core Idea
One hadith, two interpretations: Hanafi sees the prohibition as advisory, Shafi'i as absolute.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute over selling fruits before they ripen. The Hanafi school considers the sale valid but disliked, while the Shafi'i school deems it invalid. The difference hinges on how each school interprets the same prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Recall that the Hanafi school permits selling unripe fruits but with dislike (makruh tanzih), and requires immediate cutting of the fruit after the sale.
The Hanafi position is based on the hadith of Zayd ibn Thabit and the principle that the seller owns the fruit.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that such a sale is not permissible (ja'iz nahi), meaning it is invalid (batil or fasid).
This is the view of Imam al-Shafi'i as stated in the passage.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i evidence is the general hadith from Bukhari and Muslim prohibiting the sale of fruits until ripeness appears. They take this prohibition at face value as a clear legal command.
The hadith is cited: 'The Prophet prohibited selling fruits until their ripeness appears.'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that the prohibition was due to a specific cause (frequent disputes) and is thus advisory, not a definitive legal command. The hadith of Zayd ibn Thabit clarifies this.
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition can be interpreted as karahah (dislike) when there is evidence of a specific cause.
5
Determine the practical difference
In the Hanafi school, the sale is valid but makruh; in the Shafi'i school, it is invalid. This affects contracts for crops before harvest.
Hanafi muftis may advise against it but uphold the contract if executed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدہ امام شافعی کا مسلک یہ ہے کہ پھل کارآمد ہونے سے پہلے پہچنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ ان کی دلیل اوپر کی حدیث ہے جن میں کارآمد ہونے سے پہلے پھل بیچنے سے منع فرمایا گیا ہے۔ (بخاری شریف نمبر ۲۱۹۸ ر مسلم شریف نمبر ۱۵۳۵) حدیث اوپر گزر چکی ہے۔
Benefit: The school of Imam Shafi'i is that selling fruits before they become useful is not permissible. Their evidence is the hadith mentioned above in which selling fruits before they become useful is prohibited. (Bukhari no. 2198 and Muslim no. 1535). The hadith has passed.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a sidebar (fa'idah) that presents the Shafi'i school's position on selling fruits before they become useful. Unlike the Hanafi school which allows such sales (though disliked), Imam al-Shafi'i considers it prohibited (jā'iz nahī). Their evidence is the same hadith from Bukhari and Muslim that the Prophet prohibited selling fruits until their ripeness appears. The Shafi'is take this prohibition at face value as a clear legal command, while the Hanafis interpret it as advisory due to the context of frequent disputes.

Key Terms
مسلک (madhhab): Legal school; school of thought
دلیل (dalil): Evidence; proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible and recommended
B) Permissible but disliked
C) Not permissible (invalid)
D) Only allowed for certain fruits

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling unripe fruits (though disliked) based on an advisory interpretation of the prohibition hadith, unlike the Shafi'i school which takes it literally.

Selling Fruit with Exception and Conditional Stipulations: Hanafi Rulings
Prohibition of selling fruit with unknown exception and conditional stipulations with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit with an unknown exception and on attaching a beneficial condition, and distinguish between valid and fasid sales based on the proofs.

Core Idea
An unknown exception or a beneficial condition in a fruit sale makes it fasid – the sold item must be known and the sale free of extra conditions.

This lesson examines two prohibitions in fruit sales: exempting a known quantity when the total is unknown, and stipulating a condition that benefits one party. Both render the sale fasid (voidable) according to the Hanafi school. We will explore the proofs from the Sunnah and understand the difference between a permissible later arrangement and a prohibited stipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibited scenarios
The passage discusses selling fruit with an unknown exception and stipulating a condition to leave fruit on tree. Note that both lead to fasid.
2
Locate the dalil for each
For conditions, hadith 'no two conditions in a sale' (Tirmidhi). For exception, hadith 'no istithna' unless known' (Abu Dawud). Also reference to 'no profit from what is not guaranteed'.
Hadith references are provided in the source.
3
Understand the illa (effective cause)
For conditions, the condition benefits one party. For exception, it creates ignorance (jahalah) in the sold item.
Jahalah is the key reason for prohibition.
4
Apply the distinction between stipulation and permission
If the condition is not stipulated at sale but later agreed, it is permissible. Also if exception leaves sold item known, it is valid.
This shows the nuance in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i position
Shafi'is prohibit selling fruit before ripeness absolutely; Hanafis allow it with caveats (e.g., sold with tree or covering known).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different hadith interpretations.

Source Text

شافعی کا مسلک یہ ہے کہ پھل کارآمد ہونے سے پہلے پہچنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ ان کی دلیل اوپر کی حدیث ہے جن میں کارآمد ہونے سے پہلے پھل بیچنے سے منع فرمایا گیا ہے۔ (بخاری شریف نمبر ۲۱۹۸ ر مسلم شریف نمبر ۱۵۳۵) حدیث اوپر گزر چکی ہے۔ لم سید صلاحھا : جو پھل کارآمد نہ ہوا ہو، اس کی صلاحیت ظاہر نہ ہوئی ہو۔ اور نہیں جائز ہے کہ پھل بیچے اور اس میں سے کچھ متعین رطل مستثنی کرلے۔ مثلا پانچ درختوں کے پھل بیچ رہا ہے اور معلوم نہیں اس پر کتنے پھل ہیں۔ ساتھ کہ رہا ہے اس میں سے سو کیلو پھل نہیں بیچوں گا تو ایسا استثناء کرنا جس سے باقی بیع مجہول ہو جائے جائز نہیں ہے۔ اسی طرح سو درخت کے پھل بیچ رہا ہے لیکن اس میں سے جائز نہیں ہے۔ ہاں ! استثناء کے بعد مبیع معلوم رہے تو جائز ہے حدیث میں ہے عن جابر بن عبد الله قال نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن المزابنة وعن المحاقلة وعن الثنيا الا ان يعلم (ب) ( ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی التخابر ص ۱۲۷ نمبر ۳۴۰۵ / ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی النھی عن الشیاص ۲۴۲ نمبر ۱۲۹۰) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور نے استثناء کرنے سے منع فرمایا، مگر حاشیہ : (الف) ادھار بیچنا اور اس میں دوسری بیچ کو گھسا دینا، اور ایک ہی بیچ میں دو شرطیں لگا نا حلال نہیں۔ اور جس چیز کا منمان نہیں لیا جائے اس سے فائدہ اٹھانا جائز نہیں (ب) حضور نے بیچ مزانبہ، بیع محاقلہ سے روکا اور استثناء کرنے سے روکا، مگر یہ کہ استثناء معلوم ہو تو جائز ہے۔ استثناء سے مبیع مجہور نہ ہو بلکہ معلوم رہے تو جائز ہے الا ان يعلم کا یہی مطلب ہے۔ اصول استثناء مجہور ہو تو بیع جائز نہیں۔
It is not permissible to sell fruit and exempt from it known pounds. Because the tree belongs to the seller and benefiting from the property of another without guarantee is not permissible. Previously, the hadith of Tirmidhi no. 1214, 'There is no profit from what is not guaranteed' has passed. And if the buyer stipulates that he will keep this fruit on the tree for a few days so that the fruit becomes complete, he buys with this condition, then due to this condition the sale becomes fasid. This is a separate condition with the sale in which the buyer has benefit. And attaching this kind of condition to the sale in which one of the two contracting parties has benefit makes the sale fasid. In the hadith of Tirmidhi above, such a sale with condition has been prohibited: 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar: The Messenger of Allah said: "A loan combined with a sale is not lawful, nor two conditions in one sale, nor profit from what is not guaranteed." (Tirmidhi, chapter on dislike of selling what is not in possession, no. 1234) In this hadith, 'two conditions' has the interpretation that attaching a condition of benefit to the sale makes the sale fasid. (2) In the athar, it is called riba: From 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: "Two transactions in one transaction is riba, like saying 'I will sell this for such and such on credit and such and such cash'." (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, chapter on two sales in one sale, vol. 8, pp. 138-139, nos. 14633/14636) So if at the time of sale he did not stipulate keeping the fruit on the tree, but later with the seller's permission he leaves it for ripening, then there is no harm; rather it is a favor from the seller. Benefit: Imam Shafi'i's madhhab is that selling fruit before its fitness is not permissible. Their proof is the above hadith in which selling fruit before its fitness is prohibited. (Bukhari no. 2198 and Muslim no. 1535) The hadith has passed previously. 'Lam yasid salahad' [؟] means fruit whose fitness has not appeared, its suitability is not apparent. And it is not permissible to sell fruit and exempt from it some known pounds. For example, he sells the fruit of five trees and it is not known how much fruit is on them. Along with this he says, 'I will not sell one hundred kilos of it.' So making such an exception by which the remaining sale becomes unknown is not permissible. Similarly, he sells the fruit of one hundred trees but he does not sell five of them and does not specify which five trees. Then the ninety-five become unknown. The seller will want to take the five best trees and the buyer will want to give the five worst trees. So such an exception by which the sold item becomes unknown is not permissible. Yes! If after the exception the sold item remains known, then it is permissible. There is a hadith: From Jabir ibn 'Abdullah: "The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, prohibited muzabanah, muhaqalah, and istithna' except when it is known." (Abu Dawud, chapter on takhabur, p. 127, no. 3405 / Tirmidhi, chapter on prohibition of shiyas [؟], no. 1290) This hadith prohibits making an exception, but if the exception is known, it is permissible. Footnote: (a) Selling on credit and inserting another sale into it, and two conditions in one sale, are not lawful. And taking profit from something not guaranteed is not permissible. (b) The Prophet prohibited muzabanah, muhaqalah, and making an exception, unless the exception is known. From the exception, the sold item should not become [؟] unknown but remain known; this is the meaning of 'except when it is known.' The principle: If the exception is unknown, the sale is not permissible.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on two related issues in fruit sales. First, it is not permissible to sell fruit while exempting a known quantity (like 100 kg) when the total is unknown, because this creates ignorance (jahalah) in the remaining sold item, which invalidates the sale. The reason given is that the seller still owns the tree, and benefiting from another's property without guarantee is not allowed, referring to the hadith 'no profit from what is not guaranteed.' Second, if the buyer stipulates that he will leave the fruit on the tree for a few days to ripen, this makes the sale fasid because it is a separate condition benefiting one party. The proof is the hadith 'no two conditions in a sale' from Ibn 'Umar (Tirmidhi). The commentary explains that a condition benefiting either party attached to a sale invalidates it. However, if the buyer later leaves the fruit with the seller's permission without prior stipulation, it is permissible and a favor. The author then contrasts the Shafi'i position that selling fruit before ripeness is absolutely prohibited, citing the hadith prohibiting sale before fitness appears (Bukhari and Muslim). The Hanafi view distinguishes between selling individually and selling in bulk with covering, as will be seen in the next lesson. Finally, the passage discusses the principle of istithna' (exception): if the exception makes the sold item unknown, the sale is invalid; if the sold item remains known, it is valid, as per the hadith of Jabir.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): An exception made by the seller, excluding a portion of the sold item from the sale.
شرطان في بيع (sharṭān fī bay‘): Two conditions in one sale, specifically a condition that benefits one of the contracting parties beyond the sale itself.
فاسد (fāsid): A sale that is defective due to an irregularity but can be corrected (e.g., by removing the condition). It is not batil (void).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer stipulates at the time of sale that he will leave the fruit on the tree to ripen for a few days, what is the status of the sale according to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson?
A) The sale is valid because the condition is beneficial.
B) The sale becomes fasid because the condition benefits one party.
C) The sale is invalid (batil) because the fruit is not yet ripe.
D) The sale is valid only if the seller agrees afterwards.

Key Takeaway
An unknown exception or a beneficial condition stipulated in a sale makes the sale fasid (voidable); only a known exception or a later permission without prior condition is permissible.

Sale of House Includes its Keys
Ruling that keys are included in sale of house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that integral parts of a sold item are automatically included and apply it to identify which items are covered.

Core Idea
Parts integral to a sold item are included in the sale without explicit mention.

When you buy a house, you expect the keys. Is this a legal certainty? According to the Hanafi school, yes—the keys are automatically included because they are part of the locks, which are part of the house. This lesson explores the principle that integral parts are included by default, saving buyers and sellers from listing every minor component.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sold item and its integral parts
The sold item is the house. The lock is attached to the door, making it a part of the house. The key is essential to operate the lock, making it a part of the lock. Therefore, both are integral.
Integral parts are those customarily considered essential or permanently attached.
2
Apply the principle that parts are included
Hanafi fiqh holds that a part of a sold item is included in the sale without being named, unless explicitly excluded. Apply this to the lock and key.
This principle is based on custom ('urf) and legal reasoning.
3
Distinguish between integral and non-integral accessories
Not everything in the house is automatically included. Items like curtains or furniture are not integral parts; they are separate, movable goods. Only parts that are customarily considered part of the structure or essential for its use are included.
The determining factor is what is considered a part (juz') in common understanding.
4
Note the Hanafi emphasis on custom
Hanafis rely on 'urf (custom) to determine what is integral. If custom treats an item as part of the sold entity, it is included. This flexibility allows the ruling to adapt to different contexts (e.g., keys for a car).
Custom may vary by region and time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع دارا دخل في البيع مفاتيح اغلاقها. کسی نے گھر بیچا تو بیع میں اس کے تالے کی کنجی داخل ہوگی۔ وہ تالا جو دروازے کے ساتھ چپکا ہوتا ہے وہ تالا دروازے کا جز ہو گیا۔ اور جز بغیر نام لئے بھی بیع میں داخل ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے گھر کی بیع میں تالا داخل ہوگا۔ اور کنجی تالے کا حصہ ہے اس لئے کنجی بھی بیع میں داخل ہوگی۔ اصول بیچ میں مبیع کا جز بغیر نام لئے بھی داخل ہوگا۔
And whoever sells a house, the keys of its locks are included in the sale. Someone sold a house, then the key of its lock will be included in the sale. The lock that is attached to the door becomes a part of the door. And a part is included in the sale even without being named. Therefore, in the sale of a house, the lock will be included. And the key is a part of the lock, so the key will also be included in the sale. Principle: In a sale, a part of the sold item is included even without being named.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage states a simple ruling: when a house is sold, the keys to its locks are automatically included. The reasoning is that the lock is attached to the door and is considered a part of the door, and the key is part of the lock. Since parts of the sold item are included without explicit mention, the keys are included. This is a straightforward application of the Hanafi principle that everything integral to the sold item is part of the sale unless excluded.

Key Terms
جزء (juz'): A part or component of the sold item that is essential or customarily attached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, when selling a house, the keys are automatically included because:
A) They are explicitly mentioned in the contract.
B) They are a part of the lock, which is part of the house.
C) The buyer needs them to enter the house.
D) It is a local tradition.

Key Takeaway
Integral parts of a sold item are automatically included in the sale without being named.

Who Pays for Weighing and Examining the Price?
Assignment of responsibility for weighing and examining the price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on who bears the cost of weighing and examining money in a sale contract.

Core Idea
The seller pays for measuring goods and checking the price; the buyer pays for weighing the money he pays.

In a sale, services like measuring the goods and examining the money are necessary. Who should bear these costs? The Hanafi school provides a clear rule based on benefit and custom. This lesson explains the assignment and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three services
The text mentions three services: measuring the goods (kayyāl), examining the money (nāqid), and weighing the money. Distinguish each.
2
State the ruling for each
The seller pays for the first two; the buyer pays for the third. Memorize this assignment.
3
Understand the rationale
The seller must deliver known quantity, so he bears the cost of measuring. The examiner checks the price for the seller's benefit. The buyer weighs the money he is giving.
4
Analyze the evidence
The text cites an athar from al-Sha'bi. Note that it is a weak (zanni) evidence but supports the ruling based on custom and necessity.
The athar itself may seem ambiguous, but the ruling is established.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a sale of wheat by weight and payment in silver coins. Who pays the measurer of wheat? Who pays the money-examiner? Practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجرة الكيال وناقد الثمن على البائع. مبیع کے کیل کرنے کی اجرت اور شمن کو پرکھنے کی اجرت بائع پر ہے۔ اور شمن کو وزن کرنے کی اجرت مشتری پر ہے۔ بائع کی ذمہ داری ہے کہ کیلی بیع کوکیل کر کے مشتری کے حوالے کرے۔ اس لئے بائع کو ہی کیل کروانا ہوگا۔ اس لئے کیل کرنے والے کی اجرت بائع پر ہوگی کیونکہ اس کے لئے کیل کیا ہے۔ اثر میں ہے عن بردان ابی النضر قال كنت بعث من رجل طعاما فاعطى الرجل اجر الكيال فسألت الشعبي عن ذلك فقال اعطه انت فانما هو عليك (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ۲۵۰ الرجل بیع
And the wage of the weigher and the examiner of the price is on the seller. The wage of measuring the sold item and the wage of examining the money is on the seller. And the wage of weighing the money is on the buyer. The seller's responsibility is that he must measure the sold item and hand it over to the buyer. Therefore, the seller will have to get it measured. Hence, the wage of the measurer is on the seller because he measured for him. In the athar: From Burdān Abī al-Naḍr, he said: I bought food from a man, and the man gave the wage of the measurer. I asked al-Sha'bi about that, and he said: 'You give it, for it is on you.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 250, Kitab al-Buyu')
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage assigns the costs associated with completing a sale. The seller is responsible for paying the wages of the person who measures (kayyāl) the sold item and the person who examines (nāqid) the money (i.e., checks its purity and weight). The buyer, however, is responsible for weighing the money (the price) itself. The reasoning: the seller must deliver the sold item in a known quantity, so he must get it measured; the examiner of the money ensures the price is valid, which is the seller's right to receive sound payment. The buyer weighs the money to confirm the amount he pays. The text supports this with an athar from al-Sha'bi, where a buyer was told to pay the measurer's wage because it is upon the buyer (in that specific context, possibly because the buyer wanted the measurement? Actually the athar states the man (seller?) gave the wage, and al-Sha'bi said 'you give it' to the buyer. That might indicate a different scenario, but the ruling in the matn is clear: the seller pays for the measuring of the goods and examination of the price, while the buyer pays for weighing the price.

Key Terms
كيال (kayyāl): The person who measures the volume or weight of the sold goods, especially foodstuffs.
ناقد (nāqid): The person who examines the money (coins) for purity, weight, and authenticity.
اجرة (ujrah): Wage or fee paid for a service.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, who pays the wage of the person who measures the sold item (kayyāl)?
A) The buyer, because he receives the goods.
B) The seller, because he must deliver the goods.
C) Both parties share equally.
D) The market authority pays it.

Key Takeaway
In a Hanafi sale, the seller bears the costs of measuring goods and examining the price; the buyer bears the cost of weighing the money.

Selling Grain in the Ear and Beans in the Pod
Ruling on selling grain still in its husk or pod.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling grain in ear and beans in pod, and explain the proof from hadith and the response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Selling grain in its ear is permitted; slight ignorance from covering is excused.

This short but powerful passage from Hanafi fiqh establishes a key principle about permissible ignorance in sales. The ruling balances avoiding gharar with accommodating customary practices. We'll examine the textual proof and the inter-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is being sold? What is the hukm according to the Hanafi school?
The act is selling wheat in the ear and beans in the pod; the ruling is permissible (ja'iz).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith of Ibn Umar forbidding sale before maturity but implying permissibility after.
Hadith: '...forbid selling dates until they ripen and grain until it turns white and is safe from blight.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The prohibition before whitening implies permissibility after, thus allowing sale when safe.
The logical inference (mafhum al-mukhalafah) is used.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Which school disagrees? Imam al-Shafi'i objects for beans in pod due to ignorance of the commodity.
The dispute is specifically about beans in pod; both schools agree on wheat in ear.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon and why? Hanafis hold that the pod is a light covering; ignorance is excused by custom and need.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز بيع الحنطة في سنبلها والباقلي في قشرها
It is permissible to sell wheat in its ear and beans in their pod.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the Hanafi school: it is valid to sell wheat while still in the ear, and beans while still in their pod. The covering is minor, so the slight ignorance about the exact grain does not invalidate the sale. The ruling is based on the hadith narrated by Ibn Umar that the Prophet (PBUH) forbade selling dates until they ripen and selling grain until it turns white and is safe from blight, implying that selling grain in the ear is allowed once it is mature. Imam Shafi'i disagreed regarding beans in pods, considering it a sale with unknown commodity, but the Hanafi position is that the pod is a light covering that does not cause harmful ignorance.

Key Terms
سنبل (sunbul): Ear of grain (wheat, barley, etc.)
باقلي (bāqillī): Beans; sometimes fava beans
قشر (qishr): Pod, husk, shell

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling beans in their pod in the Hanafi school?
A) Permissible
B) Impermissible due to gharar
C) Permissible only if the pod is transparent
D) Impermissible unless the seller cracks open the pod

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling grain in its ear and beans in their pod, as the slight ignorance from the covering is excused by custom and the Prophet's permission.

House Sale Includes Its Locks and Keys
Ruling on what is included in sale of a house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that keys are included in the sale of a house and explain the legal rationale based on attachment.

Core Idea
Keys automatically come with the house because they are part of the lock attached to the house.

This lesson presents a fundamental Hanafi principle of sales law: when a house is sold, its keys are automatically included. The key is considered an inseparable part of the lock, which is a fixture of the house. Understanding this default rule prevents disputes over accessories.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Read the Arabic text and its translation: 'And whoever sells a house, the keys of its locks are included in the sale.' Recognize that the rule is unconditional, operating automatically without the need for explicit mention.
2
Understand the rationale
The key is part (juz') of the lock, and the lock is attached to the door of the house. Because the lock is a fixture, its accessory (the key) is also considered part of the property. This reflects the principle that attached accessories follow the principal object.
Attachment (ittisal) is the key concept.
3
Apply the rule
In a sale of a house, the seller must deliver the keys without separate negotiation. If the seller withholds them, the buyer has the right to demand them. This rule is a default; parties can stipulate otherwise by explicit agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع دارا دخل في البيع مفاتيح اغلاقها
And whoever sells a house, the keys of its locks are included in the sale.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This is a foundational rule: when a house is sold, everything that is part of it, including its locks and keys, is automatically included even if not explicitly mentioned. The key is considered a part (juz') of the lock, and the lock is part of the house because it is attached to the door. Therefore, the key is included without needing separate mention. This rule applies to all similar accessories that are integral to the property.

Key Terms
مفاتيح اغلاقها (mafātīḥ aghlāqihā): Keys of its locks

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person sells a house without mentioning the keys, are the keys included in the sale?
A) Yes, because they are part of the house
B) No, because they were not mentioned
C) Only if the buyer demands them
D) It depends on local custom

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the sale of a house automatically includes its locks and keys.

Allocation of Wages for Measurer, Examiner, and Weigher in a Sale
Allocation of labor costs in a sale transaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears the wage of the measurer, examiner, and weigher in a sale and explain the supporting evidence based on practice (athar).

Core Idea
The seller pays for measuring and examining the price; the buyer pays for weighing his payment.

This lesson addresses a practical aspect of sales transactions: which party bears the cost of ancillary labor? The Hanafi school assigns these costs based on responsibility for the respective tasks. We will examine the ruling and the evidence from the practice of the Prophet (PBUH) and the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three roles
The passage mentions a measurer (kayyāl), an examiner of the price (nāqid al-thaman), and a weigher of the price (wazzān). Determine what each does.
2
State the allocation
Who pays each? Seller pays measurer and examiner; buyer pays weigher.
3
Examine the basis
Why is this allocation made? Because the seller is responsible for delivering the measured commodity and ensuring the price is genuine; the buyer is responsible for paying the correct weight.
4
Consider the evidence
The proof from the hadith of cupping and the athar of Shu'bi. Understand how the evidence supports the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجرة الكيال وناقد الثمن على البائع واجرة وزان الثمن على المشترى
And the wage of the measurer and the examiner of the price is upon the seller, and the wage of the weigher of the price is upon the buyer.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage allocates costs in a sale. The seller must deliver the commodity measured (if sold by volume) and must ensure the price (gold/silver) is genuine (examined). Therefore, the seller pays the measurer and the examiner. The buyer, on the other hand, must pay the price, and when paying, he must weigh it (if paid in coin), so the buyer pays the weigher. The proof is the practice of the Prophet (PBUH) who himself paid the cupper's wage, and the athar from Shu'bi that the seller pays the measurer. The principle: the one for whom the work is done bears the wage.

Key Terms
كيال (kayyāl): Measurer (one who measures grain by volume)
ناقد الثمن (nāqid al-thaman): Examiner of the price (checks for genuineness of coins)
وزان (wazzān): Weigher (weighs the price coins)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who pays the wage of the person who examines the coins (price) for authenticity?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Both equally
D) It depends on agreement

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the seller pays the measurer and the examiner of the price; the buyer pays the weigher of the price.

6 min
Order of Performance in a Sale: Price First, Then Delivery
Order of payment and delivery in a sale contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the order of payment and delivery in a sale and identify the hadith proof that supports it.

Core Idea
In a sale, the buyer must pay first because the price is unspecified until paid; the commodity is already specified.

This short passage from Al-Sharh al-Samari addresses a practical question: when a sale is agreed but no order of performance is specified, who must act first? The Hanafi school, based on the sunnah, orders the buyer to pay the price first, and only then must the seller deliver. The rationale lies in the nature of the price (thaman) being generic until physically handed over.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? The order of performing the two obligations in a sale: payment of price and delivery of the commodity. The ruling is that the buyer must pay first.
This is a judicial order (wajib fi al-qada), not a condition of validity.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'الذهب بالفضة ربا إلا هاء وهاء' (Gold for silver is riba unless hand-to-hand). This hadith, though about riba, indicates the principle of immediate exchange in sales.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawood and Tirmidhi.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'هاء وهاء' (hand-to-hand) implies that both considerations must be given promptly. In a sale, one consideration (the commodity) is already specific; the other (the price) becomes specific only upon payment. Therefore, the buyer must pay first to make the price specific.
Thaman is unspecified until possession; mabi' is specified from the start.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the parties dispute, the judge will order the buyer to pay first. If the buyer refuses, the seller is not compelled to deliver. This is the operative ruling in court.
If both agree voluntarily, any order is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع سلعة بثمن قيل للمشترى ادفع الثمن اولا فاذا دفع قيل للبائع سلم المبيع
And whoever sells a commodity for a price, it is said to the buyer: pay the price first, and when he pays, it is said to the seller: deliver the sold item.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This ruling determines the order of performance when there is no agreement otherwise. The buyer must present the price first because the price (thaman) is not specifically determined until it is given – dirhams and dinars become specific only upon possession. The object of sale (mabi') is already specific from the start. So to avoid dispute, the buyer is ordered to pay first, and then the seller must deliver. This is established in case of disagreement; if both agree voluntarily, any order is fine. The proof is the hadith about riba in gold and silver: hand-to-hand transaction, indicating immediate exchange. Also the general principle that the one who is to fulfill a non-specific obligation should do so first to make it specific.

Key Terms
سلعة (sila'): Commodity, the object of sale
ثمن (thaman): Price; in this context, dirhams/dinars which are non-specific until possession

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a commodity is sold for a price (dirhams/dinars), who is ordered to perform first if they dispute?
A) The seller must deliver first
B) The buyer must pay first
C) Both simultaneously
D) It is up to the judge's discretion

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school orders the buyer to pay first in a sale because the price is unspecified until paid, ensuring clarity and preventing dispute.
